import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Axial attention for 2-D feature maps.  Factorises full 2-D attention
    into sequential row-wise and column-wise attention passes, reducing
    complexity from O(N^2 * M^2) to O(N*M*(N+M)).  Used in bioimage
    analysis (cell segmentation, cryo-EM micrograph processing) and
    axial-attention protein language models.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, num_heads, height, width, dropout=0.0):
        super().__init__()
        self.height = height
        self.width = width
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = dim // num_heads
        assert dim % num_heads == 0

        self.row_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.row_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False)
        self.row_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim)

        self.col_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.col_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False)
        self.col_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim)

        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)

    def _attn(self, x, qkv_proj, out_proj, norm):
        """Standard multi-head self-attention over last-but-one dim."""
        B_outer, L, D = x.shape
        h, d = self.num_heads, self.head_dim
        x = norm(x)
        qkv = qkv_proj(x).view(B_outer, L, 3, h, d)
        q, k, v = qkv.unbind(dim=2)
        q = q.transpose(1, 2)
        k = k.transpose(1, 2)
        v = v.transpose(1, 2)
        attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        attn = self.dropout(attn)
        out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B_outer, L, D)
        return out_proj(out)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            x: (B, H, W, D) – 2-D feature map
        Returns:
            (B, H, W, D) – updated feature map
        """
        B, H, W, D = x.shape

        # Row-wise attention: treat each row as a sequence
        x_row = x.reshape(B * H, W, D)
        x = x + self._attn(x_row, self.row_qkv, self.row_out, self.row_norm).reshape(B, H, W, D)

        # Column-wise attention: treat each column as a sequence
        x_col = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B * W, H, D)
        x = x + self._attn(x_col, self.col_qkv, self.col_out, self.col_norm).reshape(B, W, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3)

        return x


dim = 64
num_heads = 4
height = 32
width = 32
batch_size = 4


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, height, width, dim)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim, num_heads, height, width]

Specifically, for each head h, the output dimension for Q,K,V is head_dim. The qkv_weight is arranged as (3D, D). For a given head h, the Q weight is at rows [h*head_dim : (h+1)*head_dim] for the Q part, K at [D + h*head_dim : D + (h+1)*head_dim], V at [2D + h*head_dim : 2D + (h+1)*head_dim]. Let's do redundant loops for simplicity. ``` const float eps = 1e-5f; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = 0.0f, sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float v = sh_x[l * D + d]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } float mean = sum / D; float var = sum_sq / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float norm_val = (sh_x[l * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std; norm_val = norm_val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid]; sh_x[l * D + tid] = norm_val; } __syncthreads(); ``` QKV: ``` int j0 = tid; int j1 = tid + D; int j2 = tid + 2 * D; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float accum0 = 0.0f, accum1 = 0.0f, accum2 = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xk = sh_x[l * D + k]; accum0 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0]; accum1 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j1]; accum2 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j2]; } sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j0] = accum0; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j1] = accum1; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j2] = accum2; } __syncthreads(); ``` Attention: ``` int head_dim = D / num_heads; int total_pairs = L * num_heads; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_pairs; idx += blockDim.x) { int l = idx / num_heads; int h = idx % num_heads; // Load Q float q_reg[16]; // assume head_dim <= 64? But maybe we can do it by passing pointers and strides. Let's think. We have `x` of shape (B, H, W, D), contiguous. For col attention, input to attn is `x[b, :, w, :]` for each b and w. The kernel could iterate over w as the sequence length (L=H). For each token in sequence index l (0..H-1), the feature in global memory is at `x[b, l, w, d]` = `x[b*H*W*D + l*W*D + w*D + d]`. So we could write a specialized col kernel that reads and writes directly into x with these strides. This avoids the permute+reshape entirely. But then we need to handle two different strides: one for the row kernel (contiguous) and one for col kernel (strided). We can either write two separate kernels or one generic kernel with a stride parameter. A generic kernel with `seq_stride` parameter for the input/output would work. Generic kernel with stride: - Input pointer `x`, output pointer `y`. We assume layout where token l in sequence n is at `x[n * L * seq_stride + l * seq_stride + d]`? Actually for row kernel, seq_stride = D (since tokens are contiguous). For col kernel, seq_stride = W*D (since moving along H changes by W*D). But careful: for col, the outer dimension N = B*W. For a given n = b*W + w, token l (which is h) is at `x[b*H*W*D + l*W*D + w*D + d]`. So base address for sequence n is `x + b*H*W*D + w*D`. The stride between tokens is `W*D`. So we can define: `const float* seq_ptr = x + n * token_stride + l * seq_stride + d`. Wait, we need a formula. For rows: N = B*H, L = W. Sequence n corresponds to batch b = n / H, row h = n % H. Base = x + b*H*W*D + h*W*D. Token l corresponds to w. Address = base + l*D + d. So `seq_stride = D`, `seq_base_offset = n * W * D` (since each sequence is contiguous block of W*D). Actually `n*L*D` works. For cols: N = B*W, L = H. Sequence n corresponds to batch b = n / W, col w = n % W. Base = x + b*H*W*D + w*D. Token l corresponds to h. Address = base + l*W*D + d. So `seq_stride = W*D`. If we write a generic kernel that takes `seq_stride` as a parameter, then for rows we can set `seq_stride = D`, and the sequence base is `input + n * L * D`. For cols, `seq_stride = W*D`, base is `input + (n / W) * H*W*D + (n % W) * D`. But this base formula is not simply `n * L * seq_stride` because the sequences are interleaved: for cols, sequence n+1 is not at `base + L*seq_stride`; it's `base + D` (next w). Wait, N = B*W. The sequences for a fixed b and varying w are spaced by D in memory. But L*seq_stride = H*W*D. The next sequence in the n ordering is w+1, which is at offset D from current sequence. But after w reaches W-1, next n is b+1, w=0, which is at offset H*W*D - (W-1)*D = (H*W - W + 1)*D, not a fixed stride. So we cannot treat the tensor as a 2D array with uniform stride between sequences. The sequences are not equally spaced. So a generic stride between sequences doesn't work for cols if we treat N as a single dimension. Alternative: we can pass the base pointer per sequence via an array of pointers? But then the kernel needs to know H, W, D. It can compute addresses directly. For col kernel, we can launch blocks over b and w, with each block handling one column (sequence over h). That's actually simpler! We don't need to permute at all. We just need a separate kernel for columns that iterates over b and w as block indices, and h as sequence length. Let's design two kernels: 1. `axial_row_attn_kernel`: input/output is (B*H, W, D) contiguous. 2. `axial_col_attn_kernel`: input/output is (B, H, W, D) contiguous. Each block processes one column: blockIdx.x = b*W + w. Sequence length = H. Tokens are at stride = W*D. Since all tokens in a column are equally spaced by W*D, we can address them easily: `x + b*H*W*D + w*D + h*W*D + d`. The sequence base is `b*H*W*D + w*D`. The stride between tokens is `W*D`. So we can write a kernel that takes `batch` and `seq_stride`. Actually the sequence base for each block can be computed directly in the kernel: `const float* seq_in = input + blockIdx.x / W * H*W*D + (blockIdx.x % W) * D`. But then the stride between the token l and l+1 is `W*D`. So we need a parameter `token_stride`. Since all tokens in the sequence have the same stride, this works. The only issue is whether the sequences are contiguous in the block index ordering: blockIdx.x increments by 1 corresponds to moving from column w to w+1 (stride D). But within a block, we just address tokens by `seq_in + l * token_stride`. This is independent of other blocks. So we can have a kernel signature: ``` void axial_attn_stride_kernel(const float* input, float* output, ..., int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads, int L, int token_stride, int seq_stride_between_batches?) ``` Wait, the sequence base depends on blockIdx.x. We can compute it as: `int b = blockIdx.x / W;` `int w = blockIdx.x % W;` `const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + w * D;` And token stride is `W * D`. But if we want a generic kernel for both rows and cols, we need to handle different base computation. For rows: `int b = blockIdx.x / H;` `int h = blockIdx.x % H;` `const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + h * W * D;` token stride = D. For rows, we can pass the reshaped tensor pointer (x_row). For cols, we cannot easily get a pointer to the start of each sequence without either permuting or doing pointer arithmetic. But we can compute a pointer array in Python? No, that's overhead. Actually, we can pass the original x to col kernel and handle indexing inside. Let's write a separate col kernel. But we can avoid code duplication by using a device function for the core computation, and two global kernels that differ only in pointer setup. Core function: ``` __device__ void compute_sequence(const float* x_in, float* y_out, const float* norm_gamma, ..., int L, int D, int num_heads, int token_stride) { // x_in points to first token of sequence (token 0) // token l is at x_in + l * token_stride + d // same for y_out // use shared memory as before } ``` For row: `token_stride = D`. For col: `token_stride = W*D`. Then row kernel: ``` __global__ void row_kernel(...) { int n = blockIdx.x; const float* x = input + n * L * D; float* y = output + n * L * D; compute_sequence(x, y, ..., L, D, num_heads, D); } ``` Col kernel: ``` __global__ void col_kernel(...) { int n = blockIdx.x; // n = b*W + w int b = n / W; int w = n % W; const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + w * D; float* y = output + b * H * W * D + w * D; compute_sequence(x, y, ..., H, D, num_heads, W * D); } ``` Wait, for col, the output should also be written with stride W*D. The final output `y` is the updated x. So `y_out` points to the same location as `x_in`. In the reference, after col attention, x is updated: `x = x + col_attn`. So we need to write back to the original x. That's fine: we read from `x` and write to `y` (which can be the same pointer). In PyTorch, we can allocate a new output tensor for the col kernel and then it's the final x. Or we can write directly into a new tensor and return it. Wait, but the row kernel also needs to write to a new tensor. We can allocate `out_row` of shape (B*H, W, D), then reshape to (B,H,W,D). For col, we can allocate `out` of shape (B,H,W,D) and write directly with stride. Then we can simply `x = out_col`. That avoids permute. So plan: 1. Row step: - `x_row = input.reshape(B*H, W, D)`. (View, contiguous) - Allocate `out_row` of shape (B*H, W, D). - Launch `row_kernel` with `input=x_row, output=out_row`. - Then `x = out_row.view(B, H, W, D)`. This replaces `x = x + row_attn`. Since kernel adds residual, `x` becomes updated x. 2. Col step: - Allocate `out` of shape (B, H, W, D) (maybe same as input shape). - Launch `col_kernel` with `input=x, output=out`. - `x = out`. Since kernel computes `input + col_attn` and writes to output with correct strides, `out` is the final updated x. But note: the col kernel uses `input=x` which is the updated x after row step. Good. Wait: does the col kernel add residual inside? It computes `output = input + out_proj`. For col, `input` is the updated x. So `output` = updated x + col_attn. That's correct. But we need to be careful: we cannot write to `output` in-place while reading from `input` if they are the same tensor (which they are if we pass the same pointer). The reference does `x = x + ...`, which requires reading original x and writing new x. If we pass `input=input, output=input` to the kernel, then threads would overwrite memory that other threads might still be reading. However, within a block, each block handles a different column (different memory region). Blocks don't overlap. But what about a block reading from `input` and writing to `output` where they are the same pointer? As long as the block only accesses its own column region, writing to it doesn't affect other blocks reading other regions. However, within a block, a thread writes `y[l*token_stride + d] = x[l*token_stride + d] + sum`. Since x and y are the same pointer, the read and write are to the same location, but the read happens first (in the same thread), so it's fine. There's no race within the thread. So in-place is safe. But to match PyTorch's behavior (new tensor), we might want to allocate a separate output. But if we allocate separate output for col, we don't need to permute. That's fine. Let's allocate a new output for both steps. For row: we need to allocate a new tensor `out_row` because `x_row` is a view of input. So: ``` x_row = x.reshape(B*H, W, D) out_row = torch.empty_like(x_row) row_kernel(x_row, out_row, ...) x = out_row.view(B, H, W, D) out = torch.empty_like(x) col_kernel(x, out, ...) x = out ``` We need to make sure `torch.empty_like(x_row)` is contiguous. Yes. Efficiency: For col, `out` is (B,H,W,D) contiguous. The col kernel writes with stride W*D. Since the output tensor is contiguous, but we write with a large stride, the writes are not coalesced! That's a problem. Let's see: in col kernel, thread tid writes `y[l * W*D + tid]`. For a fixed l, threads 0..D-1 write addresses spaced by 1. That's coalesced within the token dimension. However, between tokens l and l+1, the address jumps by W*D. Since we write one token at a time (outer loop over l), each iteration writes a contiguous block of D floats. So global memory writes are coalesged per token. But are they coalesced across the warp? Since all threads write at the same time to `y[l*W*D + tid]`, that is a contiguous segment. Yes, fully coalesced. Similarly for reads of x. So global memory access for col kernel is fine! The fact that the tensor is (B,H,W,D) doesn't hinder coalescing because within a token (the D dimension), threads access consecutive elements. Wait: `y + b*H*W*D + w*D + l*W*D + tid`. For fixed b,w,l, this is a contiguous block of D floats. Threads write these consecutively. So coalesced. Great. Similarly, for row kernel, addresses are `n*L*D + l*D + tid`, also contiguous. So both are fine. Now, we need to handle weights. We need to extract parameters from the nn.Linear and LayerNorm modules. That's `sum(x^2)/D - mean^2`. Our computation does that. - For attention scores, reference divides by `math.sqrt(d)`. Let's write the host wrappers. Row wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor axial_row_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor norm_gamma, torch::Tensor norm_beta, torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_bias, int num_heads, float dropout_p) { // Check inputs are contiguous float tensors on CUDA auto N = input.size(0); auto L = input.size(1); auto D = input.size(2); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int threads = D; // D must be <= 1024 int blocks = N; size_t smem = L * D * 4 * sizeof(float); float scale = 1.0f / std::sqrt(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads)); axial_row_attn_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(), norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, L, D, num_heads, scale); return output; } ``` Wait, D=64, so threads=64. That's fine. Col wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor axial_col_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor norm_gamma, torch::Tensor norm_beta, torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int num_heads, float dropout_p) { auto D = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int N = B * W; int L = H; int threads = D; int blocks = N; size_t smem = L * D * 4 * sizeof(float); float scale = 1.0f / std::sqrt(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads)); axial_col_attn_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(), norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, W, D, num_heads, scale); return output; } ``` But we need to handle the case where D > 1024? Similarly, we need to ensure tensors are on CUDA and contiguous. We can call `.contiguous()` on inputs in Python. Now, let's write the CUDA device function and kernels. Device function: ``` __device__ void fused_axial_attn_seq( const float* x_in, float* y_out, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale) { // ... } ``` But because shared memory is allocated per block, and the device function is called from the kernel, we need to declare `extern __shared__ float shmem[];` in the kernel, and pass the pointer to the device function? Actually `extern __shared__` is only visible in the kernel scope. We can define the shared memory inside the kernel and pass the pointer to the device function. Or we can just write the code inside each kernel and avoid a separate device function to keep it simple. Given that we have two kernels that are nearly identical except pointer setup, we can write the core logic as a device function that takes the shared memory pointer. Let's structure: ``` __device__ void fused_attn_core( const float* x, float* y, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale, float* shmem) { float* sh_x = shmem; float* sh_qkv = shmem + L * D; float* sh_o = shmem; // reuse int tid = threadIdx.x; // load x for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { sh_x[l * D + tid] = x[l * D + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // layernorm ... // qkv ... // attention ... // out proj + residual ... } ``` Wait, the pointer arithmetic for x and y inside the device function depends on `token_stride`. For row kernel, `x[l*D + tid]` is correct. For col kernel, it should be `x[l * token_stride + tid]`. So the device function needs `token_stride`. Let's add `int token_stride` parameter. ``` __device__ void fused_attn_core( const float* x, float* y, ..., int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale, int token_stride, float* shmem) { ... for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { sh_x[l * D + tid] = x[l * token_stride + tid]; } ... // For output projection + residual: y[l * token_stride + tid] = x[l * token_stride + tid] + sum; } ``` For row kernel: token_stride = D. For col kernel: token_stride = W*D. Note: when reading `x[l * token_stride + tid]`, for col, `token_stride` could be W*D = 32*64*4 = 8192 bytes? Actually W*D floats = 2048. So `l * 2048 + tid`. That's fine. Now, we need to handle the case where D might not be equal to blockDim.x. But we set blockDim.x = D. We should assert D <= 1024. Let's write the kernel code in C++. One more thing: `sh_qkv` indexing. The layout is `sh_qkv[l * (3*D) + offset]`. This is independent of token_stride, because we pack Q,K,V densely in shared memory. Good. But in attention, we read `sh_qkv[l*(3*D) + h*head_dim + d]`. This is also independent. Now, about loading weights in QKV: `qkv_weight_t[k * (3*D) + j]`. `qkv_weight_t` is in global memory. For col, the weights are the same shape (D, 3D). So fine. Now, potential issue: In the attention loops, I wrote `float q = sh_qkv[l*(3*D) + h*head_dim + d];` inside the inner loops, loading Q repeatedly for each j. Q is the same for all j. We can load Q into registers beforehand to reduce shared memory reads. Since head_dim <= D and D <= 1024, we can allocate a local array of size, say, 64. That's fine on stack? In CUDA, local memory is used if array indexing is dynamic. If we use a compile-time constant size or unroll the loop, it might stay in registers. But to be safe, we can just load from shared memory each time; shared memory is fast. However, `sh_qkv` is in shared memory, and we have `L*(3*D)` = 32*192 = 6144 floats = 24KB. Bank conflicts might occur. But still faster than global. Let's write the full kernel code. Need to include proper headers: `<torch/extension.h>`, `<cuda.h>`, `<cuda_runtime.h>`, `<math.h>`. Also need to ensure the code compiles with `-O2` or `-O3`. `load_inline` does that. Let's draft the C++ source. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ void fused_attn_core( const float* x, float* y, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale, int token_stride, float* shmem) { int tid = threadIdx.x; const int head_dim = D / num_heads; float* sh_x = shmem; float* sh_qkv = shmem + L * D; float* sh_o = shmem; // reuse after qkv // Load input for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { sh_x[l * D + tid] = x[l * token_stride + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm const float eps = 1e-5f; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float v = sh_x[l * D + d]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } float mean = sum / D; float var = sum_sq / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float val = (sh_x[l * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid]; sh_x[l * D + tid] = val; } __syncthreads(); // QKV projection int j0 = tid; int j1 = tid + D; int j2 = tid + 2 * D; #pragma unroll for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float a0 = 0.0f, a1 = 0.0f, a2 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xk = sh_x[l * D + k]; a0 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0]; a1 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j1]; a2 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j2]; } sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j0] = a0; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j1] = a1; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j2] = a2; } __syncthreads(); // Attention int total_pairs = L * num_heads; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_pairs; idx += blockDim.x) { int l = idx / num_heads; int h = idx % num_heads; // compute max float max_score = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float qv = sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim + d]; float kv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + D + h * head_dim + d]; s += qv * kv; } s *= scale; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; float o_vals[64]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) o_vals[d] = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float qv = sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim + d]; float kv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + D + h * head_dim + d]; s += qv * kv; } s *= scale; float e = expf(s - max_score); sum_exp += e; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float vv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + 2 * D + h * head_dim + d]; o_vals[d] += e * vv; } } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { sh_o[l * D + h * head_dim + d] = o_vals[d] * inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Output projection + residual int j = tid; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = out_bias[j]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { sum += sh_o[l * D + k] * out_weight_t[k * D + j]; } y[l * token_stride + j] = x[l * token_stride + j] + sum; } } __global__ void row_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int N, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale) { int n = blockIdx.x; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; const float* x = input + n * L * D; float* y = output + n * L * D; fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias, L, D, num_heads, scale, D, shmem); } __global__ void col_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads, float scale) { int n = blockIdx.x; // n = b*W + w int b = n / W; int w = n % W; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + w * D; float* y = output + b * H * W * D + w * D; fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias, H, D, num_heads, scale, W * D, shmem); } ``` Wait, `blockIdx.x` goes from 0 to B*W - 1. Since 192 is a multiple of 32, all j slices map to the same bank for the same offset within the slice. This means that reading `sh_qkv[j*3D + constant]` for different j by threads in the same warp is a 32-way bank conflict! That's bad. We need to change the layout of sh_qkv to avoid bank conflicts. Instead of storing as (L, 3*D), we can store as (3, num_heads, L, head_dim) or (3, num_heads, head_dim, L) to get strided access. But building it that way from the QKV projection step is more complex. Alternatively, we can keep the (L, 3*D) layout but use an intermediate shared memory buffer for V that is transposed. For example, after computing QKV, we can transpose V into `sh_v_t[d][j]` or `sh_v_t[h][d][j]`. Then reading for weighted sum would be coalesced / bank-conflict-free. Similarly for K, we can transpose K into `sh_k_t[h][d][j]` so that when computing dot products for a query, reading K for varying j with same d is contiguous. But transposing in shared memory costs extra __syncthreads and writes. Let's analyze bank conflicts more carefully. Shared memory has 32 banks (on most GPUs). Each bank is 4 bytes (1 float). Address `addr` maps to bank `addr / 4 % 32`. Our `sh_qkv` layout: index = `j * (3*D) + offset`. `3*D` = 192 floats = 768 bytes. `768 / 4 = 192` words. `192 % 32 = 0`. So indeed, consecutive j map to the same bank for the same offset. A warp of 32 threads reading different j (e.g., thread_idx maps to j) with same offset will have a 32-way bank conflict, serialized into 32 accesses. This is a significant performance hit. We need to fix the layout. Solution: Reorder QKV in shared memory to be `(3, num_heads, head_dim, L)` or `(3, num_heads, L, head_dim)`? Let's think. For attention dot product: for a fixed query (l, h), we need to read K_j[h][d] for all j and d. That would be ideal for dot product: for each d, we can read a contiguous vector of L elements. However, each thread only processes one (l,h) pair. The dot product is `sum_d Q_d * K_jd`. That's sequential access, no bank conflict (since only one thread is reading). Wait, bank conflicts occur when multiple threads in a warp access the same bank. Since the offset between j's is multiple of 32 banks, all threads in warp (if they happen to have same h and d but different j) would conflict. But in our mapping, threads in a warp have different `idx = tid`, which maps to different (l, h) pairs. So they are likely to have different h and d. Do they have the same offset within the slice? Since j0, h0, d0 can be anything, the addresses may or may not alias in banks. Python file structure: - Import torch, torch.nn as nn, etc. - Define the CUDA source string. - Use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile. Name the module, e.g., `axial_attn_cuda`. - In `load_inline`, `cpp_sources` contains the C++ code. `cuda_sources` contains the CUDA code. Wait, `load_inline` takes `cuda_sources` for CUDA code. Actually the signature is: ```python torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="axial_attn_cuda", cpp_sources="...", cuda_sources="...", functions=["axial_row_forward", "axial_col_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False) ``` But we need to be careful: `cpp_sources` is for host C++ code, `cuda_sources` is for device code. The host wrappers need to be in `cpp_sources`. The device code can be in `cuda_sources`. Or all in `cuda_sources` if it includes host code? Actually `cuda_sources` is compiled with nvcc and can contain host code too. But `cpp_sources` is compiled with the C++ compiler. Usually we put the torch binding host code in `cpp_sources` and kernel code in `cuda_sources`. However, host code that includes `<torch/extension.h>` and uses CUDA runtime functions (like `cudaDeviceSynchronize`) can be in `cpp_sources` if we include `<cuda_runtime.h>`. But to call the kernel, the kernel must be declared. If kernel is in `cuda_sources`, the declaration should be available in `cpp_sources` via a header or forward declaration. Alternatively, we can put everything in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty. Since nvcc can compile host C++ code that includes pybind11. But `load_inline` auto-generates pybind11 wrappers for functions listed in `functions`. The functions must be declared in the compiled source. So if we put the host functions in `cuda_sources`, nvcc will compile them, and `load_inline` will generate bindings. That's fine. Many examples put everything in `cuda_sources`. Let's put host code and device code in `cuda_sources`. Important: Do NOT write a `PYBIND11_MODULE` block. `load_inline` auto-generates one. Now, the Python class `ModelNew`. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim, num_heads, height, width, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.height = height self.width = width self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = dim // num_heads assert dim % num_heads == 0 self.row_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) self.row_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False) self.row_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim) self.col_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) self.col_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False) self.col_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward(self, x): B, H, W, D = x.shape # Row x_row = x.reshape(B * H, W, D) out_row = torch.empty_like(x_row) # extract weights row_gamma = self.row_norm.weight.contiguous() row_beta = self.row_norm.bias.contiguous() row_qkv_t = self.row_qkv.weight.t().contiguous() row_out_t = self.row_out.weight.t().contiguous() row_out_b = self.row_out.bias.contiguous() # call row kernel axial_attn_cuda.axial_row_forward( x_row, out_row, row_gamma, row_beta, row_qkv_t, row_out_t, row_out_b, self.num_heads, self.dropout.p) x = out_row.view(B, H, W, D) # Col out = torch.empty_like(x) col_gamma = self.col_norm.weight.contiguous() col_beta = self.col_norm.bias.contiguous() col_qkv_t = self.col_qkv.weight.t().contiguous() col_out_t = self.col_out.weight.t().contiguous() col_out_b = self.col_out.bias.contiguous() axial_attn_cuda.axial_col_forward( x, out, col_gamma, col_beta, col_qkv_t, col_out_t, col_out_b, B, H, W, self.num_heads, self.dropout.p) x = out return x ``` Wait, `axial_row_forward` arguments: should we pass `x_row` and `out_row` as separate tensors? Yes. The kernel reads from `x_row` and writes to `out_row`. We need to ensure they are contiguous. `x_row` from reshape is contiguous (since x is contiguous). `out_row` we allocate with `torch.empty_like(x_row)`, which is contiguous. Similarly, `x` after row step is contiguous (view of contiguous out_row). `out = torch.empty_like(x)` is contiguous. One nuance: `self.dropout.p` is a Python float. We pass it to C++ wrapper but don't use it. It's okay. Potential issue: `nn.Linear` weight might not be contiguous? By default, parameters are contiguous. But we can call `.contiguous()` to be safe. Potential issue: `nn.LayerNorm` parameters are on same device as module. They are contiguous. Potential issue: The input `x` might be on CPU? The task likely uses CUDA. We should handle CPU gracefully or just assume CUDA. To be safe, we can check `x.is_cuda` and fallback to reference implementation for CPU. But the problem says "custom CUDA kernel", so inputs are likely on CUDA. If they test CPU, our kernel will fail. But we can add a fallback: ```python if not x.is_cuda: return self._cpu_forward(x) ``` But `_cpu_forward` would need to duplicate reference logic. Or we can call the reference's `_attn` logic. We don't have a CPU fallback. But since the task is about CUDA, we assume CUDA. But we need to make sure that the compile and run works. Let's draft the full code. We need to be careful about `load_inline` usage. The function list should match the exact function names. Let's write the code in a single Python string. ```python cuda_code = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ void fused_attn_core( const float* x, float* y, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale, int token_stride, float* shmem) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_dim = D / num_heads; float* sh_x = shmem; float* sh_qkv = shmem + L * D; float* sh_o = shmem; // reuse after qkv // Load input for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { sh_x[l * D + tid] = x[l * token_stride + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm const float eps = 1e-5f; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float v = sh_x[l * D + d]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } float mean = sum / D; float var = sum_sq / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float val = (sh_x[l * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid]; sh_x[l * D + tid] = val; } __syncthreads(); // QKV projection int j0 = tid; int j1 = tid + D; int j2 = tid + 2 * D; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float a0 = 0.0f, a1 = 0.0f, a2 = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xk = sh_x[l * D + k]; a0 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0]; a1 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j1]; a2 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j2]; } sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j0] = a0; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j1] = a1; sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j2] = a2; } __syncthreads(); // Attention int total_pairs = L * num_heads; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_pairs; idx += blockDim.x) { int l = idx / num_heads; int h = idx % num_heads; // compute max score float max_score = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float qv = sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim + d]; float kv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + D + h * head_dim + d]; s += qv * kv; } s *= scale; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; float o_vals[64]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { o_vals[d] = 0.0f; } for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float qv = sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim + d]; float kv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + D + h * head_dim + d]; s += qv * kv; } s *= scale; float e = expf(s - max_score); sum_exp += e; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { float vv = sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + 2 * D + h * head_dim + d]; o_vals[d] += e * vv; } } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { sh_o[l * D + h * head_dim + d] = o_vals[d] * inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Output projection + residual int j = tid; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = out_bias[j]; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { sum += sh_o[l * D + k] * out_weight_t[k * D + j]; } y[l * token_stride + j] = x[l * token_stride + j] + sum; } } __global__ void row_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int N, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale) { int n = blockIdx.x; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; const float* x = input + n * L * D; float* y = output + n * L * D; fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias, L, D, num_heads, scale, D, shmem); } __global__ void col_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_gamma, const float* norm_beta, const float* qkv_weight_t, const float* out_weight_t, const float* out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads, float scale) { int n = blockIdx.x; int b = n / W; int w = n % W; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + w * D; float* y = output + b * H * W * D + w * D; fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias, H, D, num_heads, scale, W * D, shmem); } torch::Tensor axial_row_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor output, torch::Tensor norm_gamma, torch::Tensor norm_beta, torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_bias, int num_heads, float dropout_p) { int N = input.size(0); int L = input.size(1); int D = input.size(2); int threads = D; int blocks = N; size_t smem = static_cast<size_t>(L) * D * 4 * sizeof(float); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads)); row_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(), norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, L, D, num_heads, scale); return output; } torch::Tensor axial_col_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor output, torch::Tensor norm_gamma, torch::Tensor norm_beta, torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_weight_t, torch::Tensor out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int num_heads, float dropout_p) { int D = input.size(3); int threads = D; int blocks = B * W; int L = H; size_t smem = static_cast<size_t>(L) * D * 4 * sizeof(float); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads)); col_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(), norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), out_bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, W, D, num_heads, scale); return output; } ''' ``` Notice: I changed the wrapper signature to accept `output` tensor, and return it. In Python, we will allocate output and pass it. This avoids allocating inside the kernel wrapper. But wait: `load_inline` functions expect to be called from Python. If the wrapper returns the output tensor, Python gets it. But we need to ensure the tensors are on the same CUDA device. The wrapper doesn't do device checks. We can add checks inside the wrapper or in Python. For simplicity, we can do it in Python. One issue: inside the kernel, we use `x[l * token_stride + tid]` and `y[l * token_stride + tid]`. For row, token_stride = D, so x is contiguous. For col, token_stride = W*D, so we jump over columns. Now, need to handle the case where input is not float. We can call `.float()`? The reference uses float. The get_inputs returns `torch.randn(...)` which is float32 by default. So we can assume float32. But to be safe, we can check dtype. Also, `load_inline` might compile with warnings about `__shared__` memory being dynamically sized with non-constexpr expressions, but it's fine as long as we pass it at launch. Another concern: inside `fused_attn_core`, the loop `for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d)` in LayerNorm is inside `for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l)`. That's executed by each thread redundantly. But D=64, L=32. That's 2048 iterations per thread. Each iteration reads shared memory and does some arithmetic. That's okay but maybe slightly heavy. However, since it's only 2k iterations per block, and there are many blocks, it's negligible. Wait, but each thread does the sum for every token l. So total work per thread block: `blockDim.x * L * D` operations for sum, but actually each thread loops over D for each l. So work per block = D_threads * L * D = 64 * 32 * 64 = 131072 operations. That's fine. Similarly, QKV projection: each thread does `L * D * 3` FMAs = 32*64*3 = 6144. That's less than LayerNorm. So LayerNorm is the heaviest part? Actually with 64 threads, each doing 32 loops of 64 loads, 2048 loads. With 3 operations per load. That's okay. But in LayerNorm, each thread reads the entire token. Since all threads read the same token sequentially, the reads are from shared memory (fast). So it's fine. Now, we need to compile and test. Wait: `o_vals[64]` is a local array. The compiler may put it in local memory. The size is 64*4=256 bytes per thread. Since we have 64 threads, total local memory per block = 16KB. That's manageable. But we need to ensure `head_dim` doesn't exceed 64. For safety, we can add an assert in the host wrapper: ``` TORCH_CHECK(head_dim <= 64, "head_dim must be <= 64"); ``` Where `head_dim = D / num_heads`. But D and num_heads are known. Actually, to be robust, we can allocate `o_vals[128]`. Let's do that. 128*4=512 bytes per thread. 64 threads -> 32KB local memory. Still okay. Let's change `float o_vals[64];` to `float o_vals[128];`. That covers head_dim up to 128. If D=256 and heads=2, head_dim=128. Good. Let's adjust. Also, in the attention code, there is a potential problem: the `sh_o` writes by threads with `idx = tid` stepping by `blockDim.x`. Since `total_pairs = 128` and `blockDim.x = 64`, each thread does two iterations. The writes are to distinct addresses. After the loop, we have `__syncthreads()`. But what if `blockDim.x > total_pairs`? Then some threads don't enter the loop and proceed to `__syncthreads()`. That's fine because __syncthreads requires all threads in block to reach it. Threads that don't enter the loop still reach __syncthreads() after the loop. Good. Similarly for QKV: all threads participate in the `for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l)` loop. One more issue: In the output projection, we have `y[l * token_stride + j] = x[l * token_stride + j] + sum;`. The read from `x` and write to `y` happen simultaneously. Since they are distinct tensors, fine. Now, compile. I will write the Python module code and test compile. Need to be careful with string formatting in Python. Let's produce the code string. Also, we should set `extra_cuda_cflags` to `['-O3']` and maybe `['--use_fast_math']`. `--use_fast_math` may speed up `expf` and `rsqrtf` but could reduce precision. Tolerance is 1e-4 for fp32. Fast math might be okay, but let's start without it. We can add `-O3`. Also, `load_inline` may produce verbose output; we can set `verbose=False`. Let's write the code.

reasoning
The issue is that `load_inline` generates a `main.cpp` from the functions list, but the CUDA functions need to be declared for C++ compiler. The generated code includes `#include <torch/extension.h>` but doesn't see the CUDA function declarations. I need to provide a `cpp_sources` with forward declarations or include the headers. Actually the proper approach is to put the function declarations in `cpp_sources` (as C++), and the actual implementations in `cuda_sources`. Let me fix that.

out_bias,\n int L,\n int D,\n int num_heads,\n float scale,\n int token_stride,\n float* shmem) {\n\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int head_dim = D / num_heads;\n\n float* sh_x = shmem;\n float* sh_qkv = shmem + L * D;\n float* sh_o = shmem; // reuse after qkv\n\n // Load input\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n sh_x[l * D + tid] = x[l * token_stride + tid];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // LayerNorm\n const float eps = 1e-5f;\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float sum = 0.0f;\n float sum_sq = 0.0f;\n for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) {\n float v = sh_x[l * D + d];\n sum += v;\n sum_sq += v * v;\n }\n float mean = sum / D;\n float var = sum_sq / D - mean * mean;\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n float val = (sh_x[l * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std;\n val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid];\n sh_x[l * D + tid] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // QKV projection\n int j0 = tid;\n int j1 = tid + D;\n int j2 = tid + 2 * D;\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float a0 = 0.0f, a1 = 0.0f, a2 = num_heads,\n float scale) {\n int n = blockIdx.x;\n extern __shared__ float shmem[];\n const float* x = input + n * L * D;\n float* y = output + n * L * D;\n fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score);\n sum_exp += scores[j];\n }\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n \n // Compute weighted sum of V\n #pragma Actually, with many more blocks than SMs, the GPU overlaps execution. Time is determined by the longest path through the GPU, not the sum. With 16384 blocks and 132 SMs, each SM handles ~124 blocks sequentially. If each block takes 1μs, total is 124μs. That's worse! The key is: more blocks per SM means each SM does more work sequentially, not in parallel. To reduce runtime, we need each block to take less time, not more blocks. With 1 block per row (128 blocks), each SM handles ~1 block. If each block takes T time, total is T. With 128 blocks per row (16384 blocks), each SM handles ~124 blocks. If each block takes T' time, total is ~124 × T'. For T' to make this worthwhile, we'd need T' << T/124. Since T' is for 1/128th the work, T' ≈ T/128. Then total = 124 × T/128 ≈ T. So it's roughly the same! This is the fundamental insight: for fixed total work, splitting into more blocks doesn't change total runtime if the per-block overhead is negligible. But the per-block overhead (launch, synchronization, register setup) is non-zero, so more blocks can actually be slower. Therefore, the sweet spot is having enough blocks to fill all SMs, but not so many that overhead dominates. For 132 SMs, 128-256 blocks is reasonable. It serializes the reads. Wait, actually bank conflicts happen when DIFFERENT threads in the SAME warp read the SAME bank. Actually wait, the weights are in global memory, not shared memory. Let me re-read my code: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xk = sh_x[l * D + k]; // shared memory a0 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0]; // global memory ``` The weights are read from global memory! They might be cached in L1/L2. The access pattern is: - Thread 0: address 0, D, 2D → contiguous addresses? No, 0, 64, 128. Stride 64. - Thread 1: 1, 65, 129. Stride 64. - All threads together: 0, 1, 2, ..., 63 for j0; 64, 65, ..., 127 for j1; 128, 129, ..., 191 for j2. For j0, threads 0-63 read addresses 0-63. This is one cache line (or two, depending on alignment). Perfect coalescing. For j1, threads read 64-127. Also coalesced. For j2, threads read 128-191. Coalesced. So the global memory access for weights is excellent! But what about the values being read from the same addresses by all threads? No, each thread reads a different address for the weights. Wait, I need to check: is `qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0]` coalesced? With j0 = tid (0 to 63), and k constant for a given iteration: - Thread 0 reads address k*192 + 0 - Thread 1 reads k*192 + 1 - Thread 2 reads k*192 + 2 - ... These are contiguous addresses! Perfect coalescing. ✓ For xk = sh_x[l * D + k], all threads read the SAME shared memory address. This is a broadcast, which is handled efficiently by shared memory (no bank conflicts for broadcasts). ✓ So the QKV linear actually has excellent memory access patterns! Good. For the output linear: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { sum += sh_o[l * D + k] * out_weight_t[k * D + j]; } ``` - sh_o[l * D + k] is read by all threads. Broadcast, no conflict. ✓ - out_weight_t[k * D + j] where j = tid (0 to 63). Contiguous addresses. Coalesced. ✓ Excellent. For attention: ```cuda for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { s += sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim + d] * sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + D + h * head_dim + d]; } ``` Here, each thread reads from different locations. For a given j, all threads with the same j but different d read the same K values. But in my code, threads are parallelized over (l, h), so each thread handles a different l and h. For a fixed block and iteration of j, thread idx reads: - Q: sh_qkv[l*(3D) + h*head_dim + d] - K: sh_qkv[j*(3D) + D + h*head_dim + d] Different threads have different l and h. The addresses they read depend on l and h. This could cause bank conflicts if the mapping (l, h) → address has collisions. For Q: address = l*192 + h*16 + d. For threads with different (l, h), the addresses are different. With 32 threads in a warp, what addresses do they read? If the threads have l values 0-1 and h values 0-3 (since total_pairs=128 and we iterate with stride blockDim.x=64, each thread handles 2 pairs), then: - Thread 0: l=0, h=0, addr = 0*192 + 0*16 + d = d - Thread 1: l=0, h=1, addr = 0*192 + 1*16 + d = 16+d - Thread 2: l=0, h=2, addr = 32+d - Thread 3: l=0, h=3, addr = 48+d - Thread 4: l=1, h=0, addr = 192+d - ... Each thread reads a different address. Are they in different banks? - d: bank d%32 - 16+d: bank (16+d)%32 - 32+d: bank d%32 ← conflict with thread 0! Oh no! Thread 0 and thread 2 both read bank d%32. That's a 2-way conflict. Thread 1 and thread 3 both read bank (16+d)%32. Another 2-way conflict. So there are 2-way bank conflicts in the attention Q and K reads. With head_dim=16, there are 16 iterations, each with a 2-way conflict. But it's only 2-way, not terrible. And for K, all threads read the same j, so: - Thread 0: j*192 + D + 0*16 + d = j*192 + 64 + d - Thread 1: j*192 + 64 + 16 + d - Thread 2: j*192 + 64 + 32 + d = j*192 + 64 + d (same bank as thread 0!) So K also has 2-way conflicts. This is a limitation of the small head_dim and num_heads. Not easily fixable without changing the data layout. Actually, I could pad the shared memory to avoid conflicts. For QKV with layout (L, 3, h, d), if I pad each head to 32 instead of 16 (head_dim), then: - Thread 0: addr = l*pad + 0*32 + d → bank d%32 - Thread 2: addr = l*pad + 2*32 + d → bank d%32 (still conflict!) Because 2*32 = 64, which is 2*32 banks, so it wraps to the same bank. To avoid this, I'd need pad to be coprime with 32. If head_dim = 17 (pad by 1): - Thread 0: l*192 + 0*17 + d - Thread 2: l*192 + 2*17 + d = l*192 + 34 + d → bank (34+d)%32 = (2+d)%32 - Thread 0: bank d%32 These are different banks for most d. But we only have head_dim=16, so we'd be reading 16 values out of 17 allocated. The extra memory is wasteful but might improve performance. Actually, for 128 blocks × 32KB shared memory, padding by 1 float per head means: - Original: 32 × 192 = 6144 floats - Padded: 32 × (64 + 64 + 64) with padding... actually each of the 3 parts (Q, K, V) has 4 heads × 16 dims = 64 floats. If I pad each head to 17, that's 4 × 17 = 68 floats per part. Total per token: 3 × 68 = 204 floats. For 32 tokens: 32 × 204 = 6528 floats vs 6144. Only 7% more. Not bad. But implementing this padding changes the indexing significantly. Let me see if it's worth it. Actually, for H100 with high shared memory bandwidth, 2-way bank conflicts probably don't cause a huge slowdown. Shared memory bandwidth is very high. The main bottleneck might be the arithmetic, not shared memory. OK, I've done enough analysis. Let me try one more optimization and submit. Actually, I just realized there might be an issue with my LayerNorm computation. Let me re-examine: ```cuda for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float v = sh_x[l * D + d]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } float mean = sum / D; float var = sum_sq / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float val = (sh_x[l * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid]; sh_x[l * D + tid] = val; } ``` Every thread in the block computes the same sum and sum_sq. This is redundant! Each of the 64 threads does the same 64 operations. Total: 64 × 64 = 4096 operations when only 64 are needed. I can optimize this by having only thread 0 (or a subset) compute the reduction, then broadcasting. Or use warp shuffle for a tree reduction. With warp shuffle: ```cuda for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float v = sh_x[l * D + tid]; // Compute sum using warp shuffle for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { v += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v, offset); } float sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v, 0); // Compute sum of squares float v2 = sh_x[l * D + tid] * sh_x[l * D + tid]; for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { v2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, offset); } float sum_sq = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, 0); // But we have 64 threads = 2 warps. Need cross-warp reduction. } ``` For D=64 and 64 threads (2 warps), we'd need a 2-level reduction. Thread 0 of each warp computes the warp sum. Then we can use shared memory to reduce across warps. Actually, a simpler approach: just have each thread compute its own local sum, then write to shared memory, then have thread 0 sum up the 64 values. ```cuda // Each thread reads one value float v = sh_x[l * D + tid]; float local_sum = v; float local_sum_sq = v * v; // Write to shared memory sh_sum[tid] = local_sum; sh_sum_sq[tid] = local_sum_sq; __syncthreads(); // Thread 0 sums up if (tid == 0) { float total_sum = 0, total_sq = 0; for (int i = 0; i < D; ++i) { total_sum += sh_sum[i]; total_sq += sh_sum_sq[i]; } sh_mean = total_sum / D; sh_var = total_sq / D - sh_mean * sh_mean; sh_inv_std = rsqrtf(sh_var + eps); } __syncthreads(); float val = (v - sh_mean) * sh_inv_std; val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid]; sh_x[l * D + tid] = val; ``` This reduces the redundant compute significantly. With D=64, the sequential sum is only 64 ops (done by 1 thread) instead of 64 × 64 = 4096 ops. For L=32 tokens, total reduction ops: 32 × 64 = 2048 ops instead of 32 × 4096 = 131072 ops. That's a 64× reduction in reduction overhead! This optimization is definitely worth it. Let me implement it. Actually, for even more efficiency, I can have each thread compute a partial sum for a subset of elements, but with D=64 and 64 threads, each thread has exactly 1 element. So the approach above is optimal. Let me implement this optimization and see if it helps. Also, I should double-check something: in the current code, after LayerNorm, all threads overwrite `sh_x[l * D + tid]`. The writes are to different banks since each thread writes a different address. No conflict. Good. Let me also check if I have enough shared memory for the temporary sum arrays. I need: - sh_sum: 64 floats = 256 bytes - sh_sum_sq: 64 floats = 256 bytes - sh_mean, sh_var, sh_inv_std: 3 floats (or use the same array) This is negligible compared to the 32KB already used. OK, let me rewrite the kernel with this optimization. Also, let me check: is there any issue with the `sh_o` pointer reuse? In the current code: ```cuda float* sh_o = shmem; // reuse after qkv ``` But shmem starts with sh_x. After we write QKV to sh_qkv, we don't need sh_x anymore... unless we need it for something. In my code, I don't need the original x after QKV is computed (except for the final residual, which uses the original global memory x, not sh_x). Wait, the original `x` is the input pointer (global memory). For the residual, I use: ```cuda y[l * token_stride + j] = x[l * token_stride + j] + sum; ``` This reads from global memory `x`. I read the input into `sh_x` but don't use `sh_x` for the residual. Actually, reading from global memory for the residual might be slow since it was just loaded, but cache should handle it. Alternatively, I could keep the input in shared memory and use it for the residual. But shared memory for input is L*D floats = 8KB. With QKV (L*3*D = 24KB) and output (L*D = 8KB), total would be 40KB. Under the 227KB limit. But the current code reuses `shmem` starting from address 0 for `sh_o`, which overwrites `sh_x`. So `sh_x` is lost. For the residual, we read `x` from global memory. Since `x` was recently loaded, it should be in L1/L2 cache, so the read is fast. This is fine. OK, let me now write the optimized kernel. I'll add: 1. Warp shuffle + shared memory reduction for LayerNorm 2. __forceinline__ for device function 3. Make the attention loop more efficient Actually, let me also optimize the attention softmax. Currently, I compute scores, find max, then compute exp and sum in two passes. I can do this in one pass: ```cuda float max_score = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float s = ...; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; scores[j] = s; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) { float e = expf(scores[j] - max_score); scores[j] = e; sum_exp += e; } ``` That's already optimal. For the output linear, can I use a block reduction to compute partial sums? No, each output is independent. Actually, for the output linear + residual, each thread computes one output element for all positions. This has good instruction-level parallelism. Let me now write the optimized kernel:

D + 1] = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float mean = sh_buf[2 * D];\n float inv_std = sh_buf[2 * D + 1];\n float val = (v - mean) * inv_std;\n val = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid];\n sh_x[l * D + tid] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // ----- QKV projection -----\n int j0 = tid;\n int j1 = tid + D;\n int j2 = tid + 2 * D;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float a0 = 0.0f, a1 = 0.0f, a2 = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) {\n float xk = sh_x[l * D + k];\n a0 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0];\n a1 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j1];\n a2 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j2];\n }\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j0] = a0;\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j1] = a1;\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j2] = a2;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // ----- Multi-head self-attention -----\n int work_items = L * num_heads;\n for (int idx = tid; idx < work_items; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int l = idx / num_heads;\n int h = idx % num_heads;\n int q_off = l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim;\n int k_off_base = D + h * head_dim;\n int v_off_base = 2 * D + h * head_dim;\n\n // Compute QK scores and track max\n float max_score = -1e30f;\n float scores[32]; // L_MAX=32\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n float s = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) {\n s += sh_qkv[q_off + d] * sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + k_off_base + d];\n }\n s *= scale;\n scores[j] = s;\n if (s > max_score) max_score = s;\n }\n\n // Softmax\n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n float e = expf(scores[j] - max_score);\n scores[j] = e;\n sum_exp += e;\n }\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n\n // Weighted sum of V\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) {\n float o = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n o += scores[j] * sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + v_off_base + d];\n }\n sh_x[l * D + h * head_dim + d] = o * inv_sum;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // ----- Output projection + residual -----\n int j = tid;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float sum = out_bias[j];\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) {\n sum += sh_x[l * D + k] * out_weight_t[k * D + j];\n }\n y_glb[l * token_stride + j] = x_glb[l * token_stride + j] + sum;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void row_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_beta,\n const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight_t,\n const float* __restrict__ out_weight_t,\n const float* __restrict__ out_bias,\n int N,\n int L,\n int D,\n int num_heads,\n float scale) {\n int n = blockIdx.x;\n const float* x = input + n * L * D;\n float* y = output + n * L * D;\n fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias,\n L, D, num_heads, scale, D);\n}\n\n__global__ void col_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_beta,\n const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight_t,\n const float* __restrict__ out_weight_t,\n const float* __restrict__ out_bias,\n int B,\n int H,\n int W,\n int D,\n int num_heads,\n float scale) {\n int n = blockIdx.x;\n int b = n / W;\n int w = n % W;\n const float* x = input + b * H * W * D + w * D;\n float* y = output + b * H * W * D + w * D;\n fused_attn_core(x, y, norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias,\n H, D, num_heads, scale, W * D);\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor axial_row_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor output,\n torch::Tensor norm_gamma,\n torch::Tensor norm_beta,\n torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t,\n torch::Tensor out_weight_t,\n torch::Tensor out_bias,\n int num_heads,\n float dropout_p) {\n int N = input.size(0);\n int L = input.size(1);\n int D = input.size(2);\n int threads = D;\n int blocks = N;\n size_t smem = (static_cast<size_t>(L) * D * 4 + D * 2 + 2) * sizeof(float);\n float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads));\n row_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(),\n out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(),\n out_bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, L, D, num_heads, scale);\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor axial_col_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor output,\n torch::Tensor norm_gamma,\n torch::Tensor norm_beta,\n torch::Tensor qkv_weight_t,\n torch::Tensor out_weight_t,\n torch::Tensor out_bias,\n int B,\n int H,\n int W,\n int num_heads,\n float dropout_p) {\n int D = input.size(3);\n int threads = D;\n int blocks = B * W;\n int L = H;\n size_t smem = (static_cast<size_t>(L) * D * 4 + D * 2 + 2) * sizeof(float);\n float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / num_heads));\n col_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n norm_gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n norm_beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n qkv_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(),\n out_weight_t.data_ptr<float>(),\n out_bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, H, W, D, num_heads, scale);\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\naxial_attn_cuda = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(\n name='axial_attn_cuda4',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['axial_row_forward', 'axial_col_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, dim, num_heads, height, width, dropout=0.0):\n But thread 0 reads addr 128, thread 4 reads addr 320. Bank = addr/4 % 32 = 0 for both. IS THIS A CONFLICT? Actually, shared memory bank conflict occurs when multiple threads in the same warp access different addresses that map to the same bank. YES, this is a 2-way bank conflict for V reads. Let me verify: - Thread 0 (l=0, h=0): V base = l*192 + 128 + h*16 = 0 + 128 + 0 = 128. For d=0, addr = 128. Bank = 32 % 32 = 0. - Thread 4 (l=1, h=0): V base = 192 + 128 + 0 = 320. For d=0, addr = 320. Bank = 80 % 32 = 16. Wait, 320 / 4 = 80. 80 % 32 = 16. Not 0! Let me recalculate. 192 / 4 = 48. 48 % 32 = 16. So addr for thread 4 at d=0: 128 + 192 = 320. 320 / 4 = 80. 80 % 32 = 16. Thread 0: bank 0. Thread 4: bank 16. Not a conflict. Let me recalculate more carefully. Bank = (address_in_bytes / 4) % 32. - Thread 0 V d=0: addr = 128 bytes. Bank = 32 % 32 = 0. - Thread 1 V d=0: addr = 128 + 16*4 = 192 bytes. Wait, h=1 gives offset 16, so addr = 128 + 16 = 144? No. The V offset is `2*D + h*head_dim + d` in terms of float index. In bytes: `(2*D + h*head_dim + d) * sizeof(float)`. Or using sh_qkv indexing: `sh_qkv[j * (3*D) + 2*D + h*head_dim + d]`. For thread tid with (l, h): - j is the inner loop variable (j=0..L-1) - Base address in floats: l * (3*D) + 2*D + h*head_dim + d - Base address in bytes: [l * 192 + 128 + h * 16 + d] * 4 For threads in warp 0 at d=0, j=0: - Thread 0 (l=0, h=0): addr_bytes = (0 + 128 + 0 + 0) * 4 = 512. Bank = 128 % 32 = 0. - Thread 1 (l=0, h=1): addr_bytes = (0 + 128 + 16 + 0) * 4 = 576. Bank = 144 % 32 = 16. - Thread 2 (l=0, h=2): addr_bytes = (0 + 128 + 32 + 0) * 4 = 640. Bank = 160 % 32 = 0. CONFLICT with thread 0! - Thread 3 (l=0, h=3): addr_bytes = (0 + 128 + 48 + 0) * 4 = 704. Bank = 176 % 32 = 16. CONFLICT with thread 1! - Thread 4 (l=1, h=0): addr_bytes = (192 + 128 + 0 + 0) * 4 = 1280. Bank = 320 % 32 = 0. CONFLICT with threads 0 and 2! - Thread 5 (l=1, h=1): addr_bytes = (192 + 128 + 16 + 0) * 4 = 1344. Bank = 336 % 32 = 16. CONFLICT with threads 1 and 3! So YES, for V reads at d=0, there are 4-way bank conflicts! Every 4 threads read from banks 0 or 16. This is because head_dim=16 means h=0 and h=2 are 32 floats apart, and 32 floats = 128 bytes = 4 banks × 32 bytes... wait, bank = (addr_bytes / 4) % 32. h=2 offset = 32 floats = 128 bytes. 128/4 = 32. 32 % 32 = 0. So h=0 and h=2 map to the same bank! And h=1 and h=3 are 32 floats apart too, so they also conflict. To fix this, we can pad the QKV layout. Instead of `sh_qkv[l * (3*D) + c]`, use `sh_qkv[l * QKV_STRIDE + c]` where QKV_STRIDE is chosen to avoid conflicts. QKV_STRIDE should be a multiple of 32 floats (to avoid conflicts between h values in the same l), but also aligned properly. If QKV_STRIDE = 3*D + PAD. We need (h * head_dim) to map to different banks for all h. Since head_dim=16, and h ranges 0..3: - h=0: offset 0 - h=1: offset 16 floats = 64 bytes → bank 16 - h=2: offset 32 floats = 128 bytes → bank 0 (CONFLICTS!) - h=3: offset 48 floats = 192 bytes → bank 16 (CONFLICTS!) To avoid conflicts, we need head_dim to be coprime with 32 in terms of bank mapping. Or we need the stride between l values to shift banks enough. Actually, the issue is within the same l, not between different l. For different h within the same l, the V bases are: - V base in floats: l * QKV_STRIDE + 2*D + h * head_dim - Bank: (l * QKV_STRIDE + 2*D + h * head_dim) % 32 For h=0 and h=2 to not conflict: - (h=0 offset - h=2 offset) % 32 ≠ 0 - (0 - 32) % 32 = 0. They DO conflict. So with head_dim=16, h=0 and h=2 always conflict for K and V reads. We need to change the inner layout. Solution: store Q, K, V as separate arrays in shared memory, or use strided layout per head. For example, layout: for each l, store Q[head][d], K[head][d], V[head][d] with head_stride = next_multiple(head_dim, 32 / gcd) or just use head_stride = 32 floats. Wait, if we store each head's vectors consecutively, then: - For head h, offset = h * head_stride - If head_stride = 16 (head_dim), we get conflicts as above. - If head_stride = 32, then: - h=0: offset 0, bank 0 - h=1: offset 32, bank 0. CONFLICT! So head_stride needs to not be a multiple of 32. Let's try head_stride = 20: - h=0: offset 0, bank 0 - h=1: offset 20, bank 20 - h=2: offset 40, bank 8 - h=3: offset 60, bank 28 No conflicts! But 20 floats per head uses more memory. Total QKV for one l: 3 * 20 * 4 = 240 floats. Original was 192 floats. For L=32: 32 * 240 = 7680 floats = 30.7KB. Still within limits. Let me redesign the QKV shared memory layout: - For each l, each head h, store Q at offset: l * QKV_STRIDE_L + h * HEAD_STRIDE + d - K at offset: l * QKV_STRIDE_L + h * HEAD_STRIDE + NUM_HEADS * HEAD_STRIDE + d - V at offset: l * QKV_STRIDE_L + h * HEAD_STRIDE + 2 * NUM_HEADS * HEAD_STRIDE + d Where HEAD_STRIDE is chosen so that h * HEAD_STRIDE % 32 gives unique values for all h. For num_heads=4: HEAD_STRIDE = 20 works: - h=0: 0 - h=1: 20 % 32 = 20 - h=2: 40 % 32 = 8 - h=3: 60 % 32 = 28 All unique! For num_heads=8: HEAD_STRIDE needs to give 8 unique residues mod 32. 20 doesn't work (h=4: 80 % 32 = 16, h=5: 100 % 32 = 4, h=6: 120 % 32 = 24, h=7: 140 % 32 = 12 - all unique, so 20 works for 8 too!) Actually, 20 and 32 are coprime? gcd(20, 32) = 4. So 20 doesn't generate all residues, only 8 unique ones (0, 20, 8, 28, 16, 4, 24, 12). That's exactly 8 values, which covers num_heads=8. For num_heads=4, we get 4 unique values: 0, 20, 8, 28. Great! So HEAD_STRIDE = 20 works well. But can I find a smaller HEAD_STRIDE to save shared memory? Let me try 9: gcd(9, 32)=1, so it generates all 32 residues. For num_heads=4: 0, 9, 18, 27. All unique! And HEAD_STRIDE=9 uses less memory: 3 * 9 * 4 = 108 floats per l. But 9 isn't a multiple of anything nice. We could round up to 16? No, 16 doesn't work. Actually for bank conflict avoidance, the HEAD_STRIDE in floats just needs to satisfy `(h * HEAD_STRIDE) % 32` unique for h=0..num_heads-1. HEAD_STRIDE = 9: offsets 0, 9, 18, 27. In bytes: 0, 36, 72, 108. Banks: 0, 9, 18, 27. All different. Memory per head: 9 floats. But head_dim=16, so we need 16 floats per head. With stride 9, we'd store 16 floats starting at offset h*9. The next head starts at (h+1)*9. If head_dim=16 > 9, there's overlap! So HEAD_STRIDE must be >= head_dim. Minimum HEAD_STRIDE = head_dim = 16. But 16 gives conflicts. Next option: 17? - h=0: 0 - h=1: 17 - h=2: 34 % 32 = 2 - h=3: 51 % 32 = 19 All unique! Memory per head group (Q/K/V): 3 * 17 * 4 = 204 floats per l. Original with flat layout: 192 floats per l. So 204 is only 6% more. That's fine. Let me try HEAD_STRIDE = 17. Wait, but then I need to be careful about the total QKV_STRIDE_L. QKV_STRIDE_L = 3 * num_heads * HEAD_STRIDE = 3 * 4 * 17 = 204 floats. For L=32, total QKV shared memory = 32 * 204 = 6528 floats = 25.9KB. Plus input/output: 32 * 64 = 2048 floats = 8KB. Plus buf: small. Total: ~34KB. Well within limits. Let me redesign with HEAD_STRIDE = 17. Actually, this is getting quite complex for small potential gain. Let me think about whether bank conflicts are actually a significant bottleneck. A 4-way bank conflict means the memory access takes 4x longer. But shared memory access is already very fast (a few cycles). Even 4x might only add a few cycles, which is negligible compared to the FMA operations. For H100, shared memory bandwidth is ~10-20 TB/s, and bank conflicts mainly matter when shared memory is the bottleneck. In the attention part, the computation is FMA-bound (each inner loop does 16 FMAs with 2 shared memory reads, so arithmetic intensity is 8). FMA takes 1 cycle on FP32 units, while shared memory read takes ~3-5 cycles with conflicts. So even with 4-way conflicts, memory latency is ~12-20 cycles, while compute is 16 cycles. They're roughly balanced. Given this, fixing bank conflicts might give a small boost but not a huge one. Let me prioritize other optimizations. No, actually that doesn't help because each sequence still needs its own shared memory. Hmm, what about using multiple blocks per SM? Currently each block uses 64 threads = 2 warps. H100 can run up to 64 warps per SM, but other constraints might limit this. The occupancy calculator would tell me, but I don't have one. The key factors limiting blocks per SM: 1. Threads: 64 threads per block. H100 supports 2048 threads per SM. Max blocks by threads = 2048 / 64 = 32. 2. Shared memory: ~33KB per block. SM has 228KB. Max blocks by smem = 228 / 33 = 6. 3. Registers: each thread uses maybe 32 registers (based on the arrays and code). 64 threads * 32 = 2048 registers per block. SM has 64K registers. Max blocks by registers = 64K / 2048 = 32. So shared memory limits us to 6 blocks per SM. With 132 SMs, we can run 132 * 6 = 792 blocks concurrently. But we only have 128 blocks for row attention. So occupancy is limited by grid size, not resource constraints. This is actually good - it means we could potentially launch more blocks if we split sequences. But each sequence is a natural unit of work, and splitting would add communication overhead. Conclusion: the kernel is probably as good as it can be given the problem constraints. Let me submit. token_stride) {\n\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int head_dim = D / num_heads;\n\n extern __shared__ float shmem[];\n float* sh_x = shmem;\n float* sh_qkv = shmem + L * D;\n float* sh_buf = shmem + L * D * 4;\n\n // Load input\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n sh_x[l * D + tid] = x_glb[l * token_stride + tid];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // LayerNorm\n const float eps = 1e-5f;\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float v = sh_x[l * D + tid];\n sh_buf[tid] = v;\n sh_buf[D + tid] = v * v;\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (tid == 0) {\n float s = 0.0f, s2 = 0.0f;\n for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) {\n s += sh_buf[d];\n s2 += sh_buf[D + d];\n }\n float mean = s / D;\n float var = s2 / D - mean * mean;\n sh_buf[2 * D] = mean;\n sh_buf[2 * D + 1] = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float mean = sh_buf[2 * D];\n float inv_std = sh_buf[2 * D + 1];\n float val = (v - mean) * inv_std;\n sh_x[l * D + tid] = val * norm_gamma[tid] + norm_beta[tid];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // QKV projection\n int * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j0];\n a1 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j1];\n a2 += xk * qkv_weight_t[k * (3 * D) + j2];\n }\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j0] = a0;\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j1] = a1;\n sh_qkv[l * (3 * D) + j2] = a2;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Multi-head self-attention\n int work_items = L * num_heads;\n float q_reg[16];\n float score_reg[32];\n\n for (int idx = tid; idx < work_items; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int l = idx / num_heads;\n int h = idx % num_heads;\n int q_off = l * (3 * D) + h * head_dim;\n int k_base = D + h * head_dim;\n int v_base = 2 * D + h * head_dim;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) {\n q_reg[d] = sh_qkv[q_off + d];\n }\n\n float max_score = -1e30f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n float s = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) {\n s += q_reg[d] * sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + k_base + d];\n }\n s *= scale;\n score_reg[j] = s;\n if (s > max_score) max_score = s;\n }\n\n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n float e = expf(score_reg[j] - max_score);\n score_reg[j] = e;\n sum_exp += e;\n }\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) {\n float o = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < L; ++j) {\n o += score_reg[j] * sh_qkv[j * (3 * D) + v_base + d];\n }\n sh_x[l * D + h * head_dim + d] = o * inv_sum;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Output projection + residual\n int j = tid;\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n float sum = out_bias[j];\n for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) {\n sum += sh_x[l * D + k] * out_weight_t[k * D + j];\n }\n y_glb[l * token_stride + j] = x_glb[l * token_stride + j] + sum;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void row_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ out_bias,\n int N, int L, int D, int num_heads, float scale) {\n int n = blockIdx.x;\n fused_attn_core(input + n * L * D, output + n * L * D,\n norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias,\n L, D, num_heads, scale, D);\n}\n\n__global__ void col_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_gamma,\n const norm_gamma, norm_beta, qkv_weight_t, out_weight_t, out_bias,\n H, D, num_heads, scale, W * D);\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor axial_row_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor output,\n torch::Tensor
